import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a causal 1D convolution: left-padded so each output only depends on current and past inputs.
    Used in autoregressive sequence models (Mamba, Hyena, WaveNet, TCN) for causal sequence processing.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of input channels.
        out_channels (int): Number of output channels.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the convolution kernel.
        dilation (int, optional): Dilation rate. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If True, adds a learnable bias. Defaults to False.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, dilation: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.causal_padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation
        self.conv1d = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        x = torch.nn.functional.pad(x, (self.causal_padding, 0))
        return self.conv1d(x)

batch_size = 32
in_channels = 256
out_channels = 256
kernel_size = 4
dilation = 1
seq_len = 8192

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, in_channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel for causal 1D convolution. Let's analyze the reference: - Input shape: (batch_size, in_channels, seq_len) - Causal padding: (kernel_size - 1) * dilation on the left side, 0 on right - Then standard Conv1d with stride=1, groups=1 So effectively output[b, oc, t] = sum_{ic, k} x[b, ic, t - k*dilation] * weight[oc, ic, k] + bias[oc] with t from 0 to seq_len-1, and x positions < 0 treated as 0. The left padding ensures that when we pad input with (kernel_size-1)*dilation zeros on left, and then do regular conv, it's equivalent to causal conv. We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that does this directly without padding. The problem sizes: batch=32, in_channels=256, out_channels=256, kernel_size=4, dilation=1, seq_len=8192. This is a fairly large compute-bound kernel? Let's estimate. FLOPs = batch * out_ch * in_ch * kernel_size * seq_len = 32 * 256 * 256 * 4 * 8192 ≈ 68.7 GFLOPs. DRAM bandwidth needed if reading input once, weight once, output once: input = 32*256*8192*4 bytes ≈ 268 MB, weight = 256*256*4*4 ≈ 1 MB, output = 32*256*8192*4 ≈ 268 MB. Total ≈ 537 MB. If GPU has ~1.5 TB/s bandwidth, memory-bound time ≈ 0.36 ms. Compute bound time if e.g. 20 TFLOPS: 68.7 GFLOPs / 20e3 GFLOPS = 3.4 ms. So actually compute-bound? Wait, 68.7 GFLOPs is significant. But conv1d in PyTorch likely uses cuDNN which is highly optimized. We need to beat it. However, kernel_size=4 is small. CuDNN's implicit GEMM or Winograd or direct conv1d (which is just a special case of conv2d) is very efficient. But maybe reference time is significant and we can win by fusing the padding and avoiding extra memory movement, and perhaps using shared memory tiling. Given the problem size, a direct custom kernel might not beat cuDNN unless we exploit specific structure. But we can try to write an efficient kernel. Let's get GPU specs first.

Let me write the kernel with the first design: ```cuda extern "C" __global__ void causal_conv1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int seq_len, int kernel_size, int dilation, int causal_padding, bool has_bias) { const int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; const int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; const int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; const int seq_tile = blockIdx.y; const int out_tile = blockIdx.z; const int seq_pos = seq_tile * 32 + lane_id; const int out_channel = out_tile * 8 + warp_id; if (batch_idx >= batch_size || seq_pos >= seq_len || out_channel >= out_channels) return; float acc = has_bias ? bias[out_channel] : 0.0f; const int in_batch_offset = batch_idx * in_channels * seq_len; const int weight_out_offset = out_channel * in_channels * kernel_size; // Process in_channels for (int c = 0; c < in_channels; ++c) { const float* in_ptr = input + in_batch_offset + c * seq_len; const float* w_ptr = weight + weight_out_offset + c * kernel_size; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < kernel_size; ++k) { int in_pos = seq_pos + k * dilation - causal_padding; float in_val = (in_pos >= 0) ? in_ptr[in_pos] : 0.0f; float w_val = w_ptr[k]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } output[(batch_idx * out_channels + out_channel) * seq_len + seq_pos] = acc; } ``` Wait, in_pos calculation: for a standard conv, output at position seq_pos depends on input at positions based on kernel. With left padding of causal_padding and conv sliding left to right: For output position t, the conv reads padded positions: t, t+1, ..., t+kernel_size-1 (with stride=1). padded[i] corresponds to original input at i - causal_padding (or 0 if i < causal_padding). So for kernel index k (0 to kernel_size-1), output[t] reads padded[t + k * dilation]. Original input position = t + k * dilation - causal_padding. If this is >= 0, use input at that position; else 0. Wait, is that right? Let me verify: - dilation: spaced out kernel. For dilation=d, kernel indices are at positions 0, d, 2d, ..., (kernel_size-1)*d. - conv reads padded[t + 0*d], padded[t + 1*d], ..., padded[t + (kernel_size-1)*d] Actually, in PyTorch conv1d: output[n, out_c, L] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} sum_{c=0}^{C_in-1} weight[out_c, c, k] * input[n, c, L + k*dilation - padding] For causal padding, padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation. So output[n, out_c, L] = sum_{k, c} weight[out_c, c, k] * input[n, c, L + k*dilation - (kernel_size-1)*dilation] For L=0, k=0: input[n, c, 0 + 0 - 3] = out of bounds (0) For L=0, k=1: input[n, c, 0 + 1 - 3] = out of bounds (0) ... With left padding, PyTorch internally handles this, but when we do F.pad and then conv, the conv itself sees padded input. For padded input, the effective formula is: output[n, out_c, L] = sum_{k, c} weight[out_c, c, k] * padded[n, c, L + k*dilation] And padded[n, c, i] = 0 for i < causal_padding, else input[n, c, i - causal_padding]. So input position = L + k*dilation. If < causal_padding, value is 0. Else original input at L + k*dilation - causal_padding. This gives: input position = L + k*dilation - causal_padding = L - (causal_padding - k*dilation) For dilation=1, causal_padding=3, kernel_size=4: k=0: pos = L - 3 k=1: pos = L - 2 k=2: pos = L - 1 k=3: pos = L Which matches our understanding! The "oldest" weight (k=0) is applied to the input 3 steps ago, etc. Hmm wait, I need to double check the PyTorch conv1d behavior. In PyTorch, conv1d slides the kernel from left to right. The first output corresponds to the leftmost position. For a kernel of size 4, if the input is padded with 3 zeros on the left, the first output sees [0, 0, 0, x_0]. The question is: does weight[0] multiply the leftmost element (0) or the rightmost element (x_0)? In PyTorch's Conv1d, weight is indexed as [out_channel, in_channel, k] where k=0 corresponds to the leftmost position in the kernel window. So: - output[0] = w[0]*padded[0] + w[1]*padded[1] + w[2]*padded[2] + w[3]*padded[3] - = w[0]*0 + w[1]*0 + w[2]*0 + w[3]*x[0] So w[3] multiplies the current (newest) input, and w[0] multiplies the input from 3 steps ago. In my formula: for output L and kernel index k: - Read padded[L + k*dilation] - For L=0, k=3, dilation=1: padded[3] = x[0]. This uses w[3] with x[0]. So in_pos = L + k*dilation - causal_padding. For L=0, k=3: in_pos = 0 + 3 - 3 = 0. This is x[0]. Good. For L=3, k=0: in_pos = 3 + 0 - 3 = 0. This is x[0] with w[0]. So output[3] = w[0]*x[0] + w[1]*x[1] + w[2]*x[2] + w[3]*x[3]. This makes sense. So yes, the formula is correct: in_pos = seq_pos + k * dilation - causal_padding And if in_pos < 0, the value is 0. Now, this is actually different from what I had in my first code sketch. I had: ```c int in_pos = seq_pos + k - causal_padding; ``` That was correct for dilation=1, but I need `k * dilation` for general dilation. OK, let me also think about the memory access pattern. For a warp, lanes compute seq_pos_base + lane_id, seq_pos_base + lane_id + 1, etc. For a fixed c and k, lanes load: input[..., c, seq_pos_base + lane_id + k*dilation - causal_padding] These are consecutive addresses (stride 1), so perfectly coalesced. Now, I realize I should also consider using shared memory for weights. Since 8 warps in a block need 8 different sets of weights (for 8 out_channels), and each set is 256*4 = 1024 floats = 4KB. Total 32KB. That might be tight for shared memory but could fit (with 48-228KB available per SM on H100). Actually, if weights were in shared memory, loading them from global memory would be done cooperatively. But since they're all from different out_channels, the loads would not be coalesced across the warp (each warp reads a different out_channel's weights). In the current design, each warp reads its own weights. Since each warp reads 1024 consecutive floats (for one out_channel, all in_channels, all kernel_size), and there are 32 warps per SM executing concurrently, weight loading will be cached well. Actually, I think L1 cache would naturally handle this since the weights are small (1MB total) and reused across many blocks. Let me write the Python code and test it. One more thing: I should also consider using tensor cores or a cuBLAS approach. But cuBLAS would require unfolding the input, which can be expensive. For kernel_size=4, unfolding requires reshaping/reindexing. We could do this with a kernel first, then call cublas, but the overhead might not be worth it. Actually, for small kernel_size, direct convolution is often faster because it avoids the extra memory traffic from materializing the unfolded matrix. Let me also think about using __ldg for read-only loads and marking pointers as __restrict__. bias.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, \ output.data_ptr<float>(), \ batch_size, in_channels, out_channels, seq_len, \ causal_padding, has_bias) if (dilation == 1) { if (kernel_size == 1) DISPATCH(1, 1); else if (kernel_size == 2) DISPATCH(2, 1); else if (kernel_size == 3) DISPATCH(3, 1); else if (kernel_size == 4) DISPATCH(4, 1); else if (kernel_size == 5) DISPATCH(5, 1); else if (kernel_size == 6) DISPATCH(6, 1); else if (kernel_size == 7) DISPATCH(7, 1); else if (kernel_size == 8) DISPATCH(8, 1); else { AT_ERROR("causal_conv1d: unsupported kernel_size=", kernel_size, " with dilation=1"); } } else if (dilation == 2) { if (kernel_size == 1) DISPATCH(1, 2); else if (kernel_size == 2) DISPATCH(2, 2); else if (kernel_size == 3) DISPATCH(3, 2); else if (kernel_size == 4) DISPATCH(4, 2); else { AT_ERROR("causal_conv1d: unsupported kernel_size=", kernel_size, " with dilation=2"); } } else { AT_ERROR("causal_conv1d: unsupported dilation=", dilation); } #undef DISPATCH return output; } ''' ``` Wait, there's an issue with the `DISPATCH` macro and the template syntax. What about the other 221 threads? They just skip and reach the barrier. That's fine. For the first few blocks where `seq_pos_base < causal_padding`, some `load_pos` values are negative, and those shared memory entries are set to 0.0f. This correctly implements the causal padding. But there's another issue: for the last few blocks where `seq_pos_base + 31 >= seq_len`, some output positions are out of bounds. I have the guard: ```cpp if (batch_idx >= batch_size || seq_pos >= seq_len || out_channel >= out_channels) return; ``` For threads where `seq_pos >= seq_len`, they return early. But they won't reach the `__syncthreads()`! This is a classic CUDA deadlock bug! If some threads in a block exit early and don't reach `__syncthreads()`, the other threads will wait forever at the barrier. To fix this, I need two approaches: 1. Make sure block dimensions exactly match the problem size so no threads are out of bounds. This works for our specific test where seq_len=8192 (multiple of 32) and out_channels=256 (multiple of 8). But it's not general. 2. Use `__syncthreads()` only when all threads participate, or have a different structure. For generality, I can handle borders by not using shared memory for the last tile, or by loading extra and having guards. But to keep it simple and safe: A common pattern is to have ALL threads participate in shared memory loads and `__syncthreads()`, and only skip the computation/store for out-of-bound threads. ```cpp // No early return! const bool valid = (batch_idx < batch_size && seq_pos < seq_len && out_channel < out_channels); float acc = has_bias && valid ? bias[out_channel] : 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < in_channels; ++c) { // All threads participate in loading shared memory if (threadIdx.x < 35) { int load_pos = seq_pos_base - causal_padding + threadIdx.x; if (load_pos >= 0) { in_smem[threadIdx.x] = input_base[c * seq_len + load_pos]; } else { in_smem[threadIdx.x] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // All threads participate in computation (or skip based on valid flag) if (valid) { const float* w_ptr = weight + weight_out_offset + c * KS; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < KS; ++k) { acc += in_smem[lane_id + k * DIL] * w_ptr[k]; } } __syncthreads(); } if (valid) { output[...] = acc; } ``` This is safe because ALL threads in the block reach both `__syncthreads()` calls. For the last block in the seq dimension or out_channels dimension, some threads have `valid = false`. But they still participate in the load and barrier. No deadlock. This is the correct approach. Let me use this structure. One more thing: `weight_out_offset` is computed using `out_channel`, which might be invalid for some threads. But the formula is: `weight_out_offset = out_channel * in_channels * KS` If `out_channel >= out_channels`, this reads past the end of the weight array. But we only use `w_ptr` when `valid` is true. So `w_ptr` is only accessed inside the `if (valid)` block. Good. Actually, `weight_out_offset` is computed before the loop. If `out_channel` is out of bounds, we might get integer overflow. But `out_channel = out_tile * 8 + warp_id`. With `out_tile` up to 31 and `warp_id` up to 7, max `out_channel = 255`, which is fine. For the last tile where `out_channels` is not a multiple of 8, some warps might have `out_channel >= out_channels`. But the memory isn't actually accessed because of the `valid` guard. The computation of `weight_out_offset` itself doesn't read memory, just computes an integer. So it's safe. However, there's a theoretical integer overflow concern with `out_channel * in_channels * KS` if `out_channel` is huge. But `out_channel` is at most ~255-1024 in practice. Let me structure the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' template <int KS, int DIL> __global__ void causal_conv1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int seq_len, int causal_padding, bool has_bias) { const int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; const int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; const int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; const int seq_tile = blockIdx.y; const int out_tile = blockIdx.z; const int seq_pos_base = seq_tile * 32; const int seq_pos = seq_pos_base + lane_id; const int out_channel = out_tile * 8 + warp_id; const bool valid = (batch_idx < batch_size && seq_pos < seq_len && out_channel < out_channels); float acc = (has_bias && valid) ? bias[out_channel] : 0.0f; const int in_batch_offset = batch_idx * in_channels * seq_len; const float* input_base = input + in_batch_offset; // Shared memory __shared__ float in_smem[40]; __shared__ float w_smem[32]; // up to 8 warps * 4 kernel_size for (int c = 0; c < in_channels; ++c) { // Load input into shared memory // Only first 35 threads participate if (threadIdx.x < 35) { int load_pos = seq_pos_base - causal_padding + threadIdx.x; if (load_pos >= 0) { in_smem[threadIdx.x] = input_base[c * seq_len + load_pos]; } else { in_smem[threadIdx.x] = 0.0f; } } // Load weights into shared memory using float4 // 8 threads load 8 float4s = all weights for 8 out_channels if (threadIdx.x < 8) { int w_out = out_tile * 8 + threadIdx.x; if (w_out < out_channels) { int w_offset = (w_out * in_channels + c) * KS; float4 w4 = *reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(weight + w_offset); w_smem[threadIdx.x * 4 + 0] = w4.x; w_smem[threadIdx.x * 4 + 1] = w4.y; w_smem[threadIdx.x * 4 + 2] = w4.z; w_smem[threadIdx.x * 4 + 3] = w4.w; } else { w_smem[threadIdx.x * 4 + 0] = 0.0f; w_smem[threadIdx.x * 4 + 1] = 0.0f; w_smem[threadIdx.x * 4 + 2] = 0.0f; w_smem[threadIdx.x * 4 + 3] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); if (valid) { #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < KS; ++k) { int smem_idx = lane_id + k * DIL; acc += in_smem[smem_idx] * w_smem[warp_id * KS + k]; } } __syncthreads(); } if (valid) { output[(batch_idx * out_channels + out_channel) * seq_len + seq_pos] = acc; } } ''' ``` Wait, for the `float4` load: `*reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(weight + w_offset)` requires the pointer to be aligned to 16 bytes. If our kernel takes ~1.5-2ms, and PyTorch's reference (pad + cuDNN) takes maybe 1-1.5ms, we might lose. But avoiding the pad might save 0.1-0.2ms, giving us a slight edge. To be safe, I should try to maximize compute utilization. Ideas: 1. Increase work per thread (e.g., compute 2 or 4 seq positions per thread). 2. This reduces the number of blocks and increases ILP. Let me redesign: each thread computes multiple outputs. If each thread computes 4 seq positions: - Thread's seq positions: seq_pos_base + lane_id*4 + i for i=0,1,2,3 - With 32 threads per warp, a warp covers 128 seq positions - Block with 8 warps covers 128 seq positions × 8 out_channels = 1024 outputs - Threads: 256. Outputs per thread: 1024/256 = 4. - Grid: (32, 8192/128=64, 256/8=32) = 32*64*32 = 65,536 blocks. Per thread compute: 4 outputs × 256 in_channels × 4 kernel = 4096 FMAs. Per block compute: 256 threads × 4096 FMAs = 1,048,576 FMAs. Total compute: same 68.7B. Memory per block for input: need 128 + 3 = 131 floats per in_channel. Load: 131 floats with ~33 threads. With many blocks. - Actually, at SM level: each SM might run multiple blocks. 4096 FMAs / (67T/132 SMs) = 4096 / 508G = 8 microseconds per block per c? Hmm, I need to think differently. The key insight: each block now does 4× as much compute per syncthreads. The ratio of FMAs to syncthreads is 4× better. This should improve performance. But the shared memory for input is 131 floats instead of 35. That's still tiny. And the access pattern changes. For thread i computing 4 positions, the inputs needed are: - For k=0: positions seq_pos_base + i*4 - 3, seq_pos_base + i*4 - 2, seq_pos_base + i*4 - 1, seq_pos_base + i*4 - For k=1: positions +1 for each - etc. These can be loaded into shared memory and accessed sequentially. However, there's a shared memory bank conflict issue. If thread 0 loads positions 0,1,2,3 and thread 1 loads 4,5,6,7, etc., and they all store to shared memory consecutively, there are no bank conflicts. But when reading: - Thread 0 reads smem[i*4 + k] for k=0,1,2,3. The 4 positions are consecutive in shared memory. - Threads in a warp read: thread 0 reads smem[0,1,2,3], thread 1 reads smem[4,5,6,7], etc. - At any given k, threads read smem[i*4 + k]. For fixed k, threads read smem[0+k], smem[4+k], smem[8+k], ... These are spaced by 4 floats = 16 bytes. If shared memory has 32 banks of 4 bytes each, then positions 0, 4, 8, 12 map to banks 0, 4, 8, 12, etc. No conflicts! (Each thread accesses a different bank). Actually, for a warp of 32 threads reading smem[i*4 + k] where i is thread index: - smem[0+k], smem[4+k], smem[8+k], ... - Bank = (address / 4 bytes) % 32 - For k=0: 0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28, 32, 36, 40, 44, 48, 52, 56, 60, 64, 68, 72, 76, 80, 84, 88, 92, 96, 100, 104, 108, 112, 116, 120, 124 - Banks: 0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28, 0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28, 0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28, 0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28 Oh! There ARE bank conflicts! Banks repeat every 8 threads. So threads 0 and 8 both access bank 0. This is an 8-way bank conflict! To avoid this, I can pad the shared memory. Instead of storing 4 consecutive floats per thread, I can add a pad: ```cpp __shared__ float in_smem[128][5]; // 128 positions, 5th column is padding // For position p: in_smem[p/4][p%4] // Or better: __shared__ float in_smem[33][4]; // 33 rows of 4, row-major with row padding to 5? // Simpler: just pad every 4 floats with 1 extra float: __shared__ float in_smem[164]; // 131 + padding = 131 + 33 = 164 // Position p stored at p + p/4 = p * 5/4 ``` Actually, a cleaner way: ```cpp __shared__ float in_smem[132]; // 131 floats + 1 pad = 132 // Position p is at smem_idx = p + (p / 4) -- no, not quite. ``` For 32 threads and 4 positions each, storing with 1 float padding every 4 floats: ```cpp __shared__ float in_smem[(128 + 31) * 1]; // hmm ``` Let me just use a 2D layout: ```cpp __shared__ float in_smem[34][5]; // 34 rows of 5 floats each. Only use 4 per row. // Position for thread i, offset j (j=0,1,2,3) is at in_smem[i][j]. // Thread i reads in_smem[i][k]. // Bank = (i * 5 + j) % 32. // For fixed j, 32 threads have banks: (0*5+j)%32, (1*5+j)%32, ..., (31*5+j)%32 // = j, 5+j, 10+j, 15+j, 20+j, 25+j, 30+j, 3+j, 8+j, 13+j, 18+j, 23+j, 28+j, 1+j, 6+j, 11+j, 16+j, 21+j, 26+j, 31+j, 4+j, 9+j, 14+j, 19+j, 24+j, 29+j, 2+j, 7+j, 12+j, 17+j, 22+j, 27+j ``` For j=0: 0, 5, 10, 15, 20, 25, 30, 3, 8, 13, 18, 23, 28, 1, 6, 11, 16, 21, 26, 31, 4, 9, 14, 19, 24, 29, 2, 7, 12, 17, 22, 27 All different! No bank conflicts! So `in_smem[34][5]` with `in_smem[i][j]` gives no bank conflicts. We need 34 rows because 128/4 = 32 threads, plus we need some overlap for the kernel_size-1 padding. One more question: should I use `__ldg` for input loads? `__ldg` is for read-only global memory with caching. Since input is read-only and we want it cached, `__ldg` is appropriate. On H100, `__ldg` uses the read-only texture/L1 cache path, which can help. But when using `__ldg`, load_pos needs to be a valid address or it might fault. Since we have the guard (`load_pos >= 0 && load_pos < seq_len`), we can use `__ldg` in the valid branch: ```cpp if (load_pos >= 0 && load_pos < seq_len) { in_smem[threadIdx.x] = __ldg(input_base + c * seq_len + load_pos); } else { in_smem[threadIdx.x] = 0.0f; } ``` I'll use `__ldg` for all read-only loads: input, weight, and bias. Yes, it's just a few extra operations per accumulation: ```cpp float sum = 0.0f; float c = 0.0f; for (...) { float y = in_val * w_val - c; float t = sum + y; c = (t - sum) - y; sum = t; } ``` This adds about 4 FMAs per iteration. In the kernel: ```cpp float acc[4] = {0, 0, 0, 0}; // for kernel_size=4 for (int ic = 0; ic < in_channels; ic += 4) { for (int k = 0; k < kernel_size; k++) { int chunk = (ic + k) % 4; // or some grouping } } ``` Actually, a simpler chunked accumulation: accumulate into `local` across all iterations, but store every N iterations into an intermediate register array. Since we're unrolling the kernel_size=4 loop, we can treat each of the 4 products as partial sums over in_channels. For kernel_size=4: ```cpp float acc0 = 0, acc1 = 0, acc2 = 0, acc3 = 0; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < in_channels; ++c) { for (int k = 0; k < 4; ++k) { float in_val = ...; } acc0 += in_val0 * w0; acc1 += in_val1 * w1; acc2 += in_val2 * w2; acc3 += in_val3 * w3; } float result = acc0 + acc1 + acc2 + acc3; ``` This sums 256*4=1024 products, but groups them by kernel position first, then sums 4 partial sums at the end. The partial sums each have ~256 terms, so error is ~256*eps. The final sum of 4 terms has error ~4*eps. Total error is much smaller than summing 1024 terms naively. Each thread reads weight for all `ic` and `k`, so all threads in the warp read the same weight. This is a broadcast. H100 broadcast is efficient (L2 cache, not from global). So we might not need shared memory for weight. For input: threads in a warp read 32 consecutive positions for each (ic,k). This is coalesced. Good. So a simple kernel: ```cpp __global__ void causal_conv1d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int IC, int OC, int SL, int P) { int lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32; int warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32; int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; int seq_block = blockIdx.y; int oc_block = blockIdx.z; int seq_pos = seq_block * 32 + lane_id; int oc = oc_block * 8 + warp_id; if (batch_idx >= B || seq_pos >= SL || oc >= OC) return; float p0 = 0, p1 = 0, p2 = 0, p3 = 0; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ++ic) { int in_base = batch_idx * IC * SL + ic * SL; int w_base = oc * IC * 4 + ic * 4; float w0 = weight[w_base + 0]; float w1 = weight[w_base + 1]; float w2 = weight[w_base + 2]; float w3 = weight[w_base + 3]; int i0 = seq_pos + 0 - P; int i1 = seq_pos + 1 - P; int i2 = seq_pos + 2 - P; int i3 = seq_pos + 3 - P; float v0 = (i0 >= 0) ? input[in_base + i0] : 0.0f; float v1 = (i1 >= 0) ? input[in_base + i1] : 0.0f; float v2 = (i2 >= 0) ? input[in_base + i2] : 0.0f; float v3 = (i3 >= 0) ? input[in_base + i3] : 0.0f; p0 += v0 * w0; p1 += v1 * w1; p2 += v2 * w2; p3 += v3 * w3; } float acc = (bias != nullptr) ? bias[oc] : 0.0f; acc += p0 + p1 + p2 + p3; output[batch_idx * OC * SL + oc * SL + seq_pos] = acc; } ``` Wait, this computes p_k = sum_ic input[seq_pos + k - P] * weight[k]. But the input index should be `seq_pos + k*DIL - P`. For DIL=1, it's `seq_pos + k - P`. For DIL=2, it's `seq_pos + 2*k - P`. This is correct. Now, the partial sums p0, p1, p2, p3 each sum over IC=256 terms. The final sum is of 4 terms. So total error is ~256*eps for each partial sum, plus ~4*eps for the final sum. The total relative error is much smaller than summing all 1024 terms naively. Moreover, cuDNN's implicit GEMM for K=1024 might tile K into, say, 32-element chunks. The final sum of partial tiles also has reduced error. So our error profile might be similar. Let's try this kernel with P = (KS-1)*DIL. But wait, I need to handle arbitrary kernel_size. The problem says kernel_size=4 for the benchmark, but the class should accept arbitrary kernel_size. I can template the kernel for different sizes and fall back to a generic loop for unsupported sizes. For the parameters in question: kernel_size=4, dilation=1. So a templated dispatch for <4, 1> is perfect. Let me write the code and test it. I'll compile first, then check correctness. Actually, maybe I should try using Tensor Cores via CUTLASS or simply via `mma` PTX. But that's too complex. One more idea: use `wmma` to do the matrix multiply in a way that exactly replicates cuBLAS/cuDNN's Tensor Core behavior? Probably not worth it. Let me go with the clean partial-sum direct kernel and test correctness. If it passes correctness, I can optimize for speed. But actually, the partial sums approach might be slower because of register pressure (p0, p1, p2, p3). However, for K=4, 4 extra registers is nothing. And it provides ILP (instruction-level parallelism) because there are 4 independent FMAs per iteration. Actually, inside the loop, after loading the 4 input values and 4 weights, the 4 multiplies and 4 adds can be partially pipelined. So it should be faster than a single accumulator. Let me write this kernel. I'll also add `__ldg` for weight reads since they're broadcast. Actually, `__ldg` for weight is good (read-only, broadcast to all threads in warp). For input, consecutive threads read consecutive addresses, so regular loads are already coalesced. `__ldg` can still help by bypassing L1 if data is not reused, but since we read 4 consecutive elements per thread that might be in the same cache line, it doesn't matter much. OK, here's the kernel code. I'll be very careful. Wait, one thing: `in_base + i0` etc. `i0` might be negative. I check `i0 >= 0` before loading. For `seq_pos < P`, some of `i0, i1, ...` are negative. For example, P=3, seq_pos=0: i0=-3, i1=-2, i2=-1, i3=0. So v0=v1=v2=0, v3=input[0]. Correct. seq_pos=1: i0=-2, i1=-1, i2=0, i3=1. Correct. seq_pos=2: i0=-1, i1=0, i2=1, i3=2. Here's a fully PyTorch approach that avoids explicit padding: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=1, bias=False): super().__init__() self.causal_padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation self.conv1d = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=dilation, bias=bias) def forward(self, x): B, IC, SL = x.shape OC = self.conv1d.out_channels KS = self.conv1d.kernel_size[0] D = self.conv1d.dilation[0] P = self.causal_padding # Create strided columns using F.pad + unfold x_padded = F.pad(x, (P, 0)) # x_padded: (B, IC, SL+P) # unfold to: (B, IC, SL, KS) x_cols = x_padded.unfold(2, KS, 1) # shape: (B, IC, SL, KS) # Reshape for matmul # x_cols -> (B*SL, IC*KS) x_cols = x_cols.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).reshape(B * SL, IC * KS) # weight: (OC, IC, KS) -> (OC, IC*KS) w = self.conv1d.weight.reshape(OC, IC * KS) # matmul: (B*SL, IC*KS) @ (IC*KS, OC).T = (B*SL, OC) out = x_cols @ w.T # Reshape back out = out.reshape(B, SL, OC).permute(0, 2, 1) # (B, OC, SL) if self.conv1d.bias is not None: out = out + self.conv1d.bias.unsqueeze(1) return out ``` The issue with this: `unfold` creates a view but `permute` and `reshape` might need a copy. bias[out_ch] : 0.0f;\n \n // Iterate over IC with shared memory for input\n for (int ic_tile = 0; ic_tile < IC; ic_tile += 1) {\n int ic = ic_tile;\n \n const float* in_ptr = input + (blockIdx.x * IC + ic) * SL;\n const float* w_ptr = weight + (out_ch * IC + ic) * KS;\n \n #pragma unroll 4\n for (int k = 0; k < KS; ++k) {\n int in_pos = seq_pos + k * DIL - P;\n float in_val = (in_pos >= 0) ? in_ptr[in_pos] : 0.0f;\n float w_val = w_ptr[k];\n acc += in_val * w_val;\n }\n }\n \n int out_idx = (blockIdx.x * OC + out_ch) * SL + seq_pos;\n output[out_idx] = acc;\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor causal_conv1d_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor weight,\n torch::Tensor bias,\n int dilation,\n bool has_bias)\n{\n int B = input.size(0);\n int IC = input.size(1);\n int SL = input.size(2);\n int OC = weight.size(0);\n int KS = weight.size(2);\n int P = (KS - 1) * dilation;\n\n auto output = torch::empty({B, OC, SL}, input.options());\n \n dim3 threads(32, 32);\n dim3 grid(B, (SL + 31) / 32, (OC + 31) / 32);\n\n causal_conv1d_kernel_v4<<<grid, threads>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(),\n has_bias ? bias.data_ptr<float>() : nullptr,\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, IC, OC, SL, KS, dilation, P);\n\n return output;\n}\n'''\n\nCPP_SOURCES = r'''\ntorch::Tensor causal_conv1d_forward(\n torch::Tensor input,\n torch::Tensor weight,\n torch::Tensor bias,\n int dilation,\n bool has_bias);\n'''\n\ncausal_conv1d_cuda = load_inline(\n name='causal_conv1d_v4',\n cpp_sources=CPP_SOURCES,\n cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE,\n functions=['causal_conv1d_forward'],\n extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],\n verbose=False\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=1, bias=False):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.causal_padding = (kernel_size - 1) * dilation\n self.conv1d = nn.Conv1d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)\n self.dilation = dilation\n self.has_bias = bias\n\n def forward(self, x):\n weight = self.conv1d.weight\n bias = self.conv1d.bias if self.has_bias else torch.empty(0, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype)\n return causal_conv1d_cuda.causal_conv1d_forward(x, weight, bias, self.dilation, self.has_bias)\n" }
